## Agent 85: CSS Scroll-Driven Animations Browser Support (March 2026)

*Source: ad2b749348c00705d.output*

I'll search for current information about CSS scroll-driven animations browser support.
## CSS Scroll-Driven Animations Browser Support (March 2026)

**PRODUCTION-READY:** Scroll-driven animations reached cross-browser baseline in 2026.

### Browser Support Matrix
- **Chrome/Edge/Opera:** Full support since v115 (2023)
- **Safari:** Full support since v18 (2024)
- **Firefox:** Experimental (behind `layout.css.animation-timeline.enabled` flag) — expected to ship soon as part of Interop 2026

### Key Properties
- `animation-timeline: scroll()` — triggers animations based on scroll position
- `animation-timeline: view()` — triggers when elements enter the viewport
- `animation-range` — controls animation start/stop points (e.g., `entry 0% cover 100%`)

### Production Recommendation
Covers ~95% of users. Use `@supports (animation-timeline: view())` for progressive enhancement. Fallback options: Intersection Observer API or scroll-timeline polyfill.

### Important Note
Firefox requires `animation-duration: 1ms` for compatibility when the feature is enabled.

## Sources
- [MDN: Scroll-driven animations](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/CSS/Guides/Scroll-driven_animations)
- [Chrome DevTools: Scroll-driven animations](https://developer.chrome.com/docs/css-ui/scroll-driven-animations)
- [WebKit: Scroll-driven animations guide](https://webkit.org/blog/17101/a-guide-to-scroll-driven-animations-with-just-css/)
- [CanIUse: animation-timeline scroll()](https://caniuse.com/mdn-css_properties_animation-timeline_scroll)

## Agent 86: ad6202817936f4958

*Source: ad6202817936f4958.output*

I'll read all 10 hooks simultaneously to perform all four audits efficiently.
All files are now read. Here are the four audit reports:

---

```
AGENT 16 — useMediaQuery SSR Audit
VERDICT: PASS

Trace analysis:
1. SERVER RENDER: useState(defaultValue) initializes `matches` to defaultValue (false).
   useState(false) initializes `mounted` to false.
   useEffect does NOT run on the server — no window access.
   `if (!mounted) return defaultValue` fires → renders defaultValue. Correct.

2. CLIENT HYDRATE: React hydrates with the same defaultValue output — no mismatch
   because the server and initial client render are identical.

3. EFFECT FIRES (after hydration): setMounted(true), window.matchMedia(query) runs,
   setMatches(mql.matches) updates to the real value.

4. RE-RENDER: mounted is now true → `if (!mounted)` branch skipped → returns real
   `matches` value.

5. FLASH PREVENTION: The `if (!mounted) return defaultValue` guard on line 29 ensures
   the component renders defaultValue (false) on both SSR and the initial hydration
   pass, preventing any hydration mismatch. No flash.

6. EVENT LISTENER CLEANUP: mql.removeEventListener("change", handler) present in the
   useEffect return. PASS.

One minor note: the `mounted` state variable is declared and used correctly but never
exposed from the hook. That is fine since callers can infer SSR-safety implicitly.
```

---

```
AGENT 17 — useTheme FOUC Audit
VERDICT: PASS

FOUC prevention analysis:
1. INITIAL RENDER: resolvedTheme initializes to defaultTheme ("light") via
   useState<ResolvedTheme>(defaultTheme) — line 31. mounted starts as false.
   The hook returns { theme: "system", resolvedTheme: defaultTheme, mounted: false }.
   No localStorage access, no document mutation at this stage.

2. localStorage ACCESS: Occurs on line 44 inside the first useEffect (lines 42–48).
   Guarded only by the effect boundary (client-only), which is correct. No SSR leak.

3. DOM MUTATIONS: Lines 55–56 (setAttribute + classList.toggle) are inside the second
   useEffect (lines 51–57), which has an explicit `if (!mounted) return` guard on
   line 52. This prevents any document mutation during SSR or before hydration.

4. SYSTEM PREFERENCE LISTENER: Third useEffect (lines 60–66) also has
   `if (!mounted || theme !== "system") return` guard. Cleanup via
   mql.removeEventListener("change", handler) is present. PASS.

5. FOUC RISK — PARTIAL: The hook is SSR-safe and returns defaultTheme consistently.
   However, the docs correctly note that callers should add `suppressHydrationWarning`
   to the <html> element, because the document.documentElement mutations happen
   after hydration and a visible theme flash is still possible without a blocking
   <script> in <head> (a common pattern with next-themes). This is a known
   limitation documented in the JSDoc comment — it is not a bug in the hook itself.

6. setTheme writes localStorage synchronously outside useEffect (line 72), but this
   is inside a useCallback that only runs on user interaction (client-side only),
   so it is safe.
```

---

```
AGENT 18 — Hook Cleanup Audit
VERDICT: PASS (all 10 hooks clean)

Per-hook findings:

useCopyToClipboard (lines 38–40):
  PASS — useEffect returns `() => clearTimeout(timeoutRef.current)`.
  Prevents setState-after-unmount on the pending reset timeout.

useDebounce (lines 15–18):
  PASS — useEffect returns `() => clearTimeout(timer)`. Correct.

useIntersectionObserver (lines 41–52):
  PASS — returns `() => observer.disconnect()`. Handles frozen (freezeOnceVisible)
  case by returning early without attaching, avoiding a stale observer.

useKeyboardShortcut (lines 51–54):
  PASS — returns `() => window.removeEventListener('keydown', handler)`.
  handler is memoized via useCallback with full dependency array.

useLocalStorage:
  NO useEffect present — no cleanup needed. State updates are synchronous.
  PASS.

useLockBodyScroll (lines 16–38):
  PASS — restores all four body style properties (overflow, position, top, width)
  and calls window.scrollTo(0, scrollY) in the cleanup. Correct scroll position
  restoration. When `locked` is false the effect returns early without mutating
  DOM, so no cleanup is needed in that branch.

useMediaQuery (lines 19–27):
  PASS — returns `() => mql.removeEventListener("change", handler)`.

useReducedMotion:
  Delegates entirely to useMediaQuery. Cleanup inherited. PASS.

useScrollProgress (lines 52–57):
  PASS — target is captured as `ref?.current ?? window` before attaching, and
  the same captured reference is used in cleanup. Correctly handles both window
  and element targets.

useTheme (lines 60–65):
  PASS — system preference listener returns `() => mql.removeEventListener("change", handler)`.
  DOM-mutating effect (lines 51–57) has no external subscription to clean up.

Summary: All useEffect hooks that attach event listeners or observers clean up
correctly. No missing cleanups found.
```

---

```
AGENT 19 — Hook TypeScript Audit
VERDICT: PASS with one minor observation

`any` types: NONE found across all 10 hooks.

Return types:
- useMediaQuery: returns boolean — correct, matches signature `): boolean`.
- useTheme: returns object literal — inferred, no explicit return type annotation.
  The inferred type is accurate and complete. Minor style note only.
- useCopyToClipboard: returns { copy, copied, error } — inferred, all fields typed
  correctly (copy: (text: string) => Promise<void>, copied: boolean,
  error: Error | null).
- useIntersectionObserver: explicit return type on lines 26–30 — PASS.
- useKeyboardShortcut: explicit ): void — PASS.
- useScrollProgress: explicit ): number — PASS.
- useLockBodyScroll: explicit ): void — PASS.
- useDebounce<T>: generic, returns T — PASS.
- useLocalStorage<T>: returns [T, setter, remover] — PASS.
- useReducedMotion: returns boolean (from useMediaQuery) — PASS.

Generic constraints:
- useDebounce<T>: no constraint on T. Acceptable — any value type can be debounced.
- useLocalStorage<T>: no constraint on T. Acceptable — callers provide concrete types.

RefObject types:
- useIntersectionObserver: `useRef<HTMLElement | null>(null)` and return type
  `RefObject<HTMLElement | null>` — uses HTMLElement, not HTMLDivElement. PASS.
- useScrollProgress parameter: `React.RefObject<HTMLElement | null>` — PASS.

One minor observation:
- useScrollProgress references `React.RefObject` (line 25) with a bare `React`
  namespace but does NOT import React explicitly. This works only because the file
  is `'use client'` and the project likely has a JSX transform that auto-imports
  React types, OR because React types are globally available. In a strict
  `isolatedModules` / `verbatimModuleSyntax` setup this could generate a type
  error. All other hooks use named imports from 'react'. This is the only
  inconsistency.

- useLockBodyScroll casts `e.target as HTMLElement` in useKeyboardShortcut (line 41)
  — this is a standard DOM cast, not unsafe.

- The `catch {}` empty block in useLocalStorage.removeValue (line 39) silently
  swallows localStorage errors, which conflicts with the project's
  "Never silently swallow errors" coding style rule. This is a style violation,
  not a TypeScript issue, but worth flagging.
```

---

**Cross-cutting summary for the swarm conductor:**

- All four audits return PASS with no critical or blocking defects.
- Three minor findings worth logging:
  1. **Agent 17** — FOUC is only fully prevented if the caller adds `suppressHydrationWarning` to `<html>` and optionally a blocking `<script>` to inject the saved theme before paint. The hook is correct but the protection is incomplete without that integration work.
  2. **Agent 19** — `useScrollProgress` references `React.RefObject` without an explicit React import (line 25 of `/Users/inspectre/Developer/web-god-pkg/examples/react-hooks/useScrollProgress.ts`).
  3. **Agent 19** — `useLocalStorage`'s `removeValue` silently swallows localStorage errors in the `catch {}` block (line 39 of `/Users/inspectre/Developer/web-god-pkg/examples/react-hooks/useLocalStorage.ts`), violating the project's error-handling style rule.

## View Transitions API State (March 2026)

**Browser Support:** Solid across modern browsers. Same-document (SPA) transitions work in Chrome 111+, Edge 111+, Safari 18+, Firefox 133+. Cross-document (MPA) support: Chrome 126+, Firefox 144+, with Interop 2026 adoption underway.

**Next.js Integration:** Native support via `experimental.viewTransition` flag in `next.config.js` (Next.js 15.2+). React Canary includes `<ViewTransition>` component. Third-party `next-view-transitions` library available for easier implementation.

**Cross-Document Status:** Working in production for Chrome/Firefox. Requires same-origin navigation and opt-in via `@view-transition { navigation: auto }`. Advanced element-scoped transitions (concurrent/nested) now available.

**Recommendation for web-god:** YES. Include as optional feature. Browser support is sufficiently broad (major engines supported), Next.js integration is native/experimental, and cross-document transitions work for MPAs. Position as progressive enhancement for page transitions in both SPA and MPA contexts.

## AI Security Threats Report (March 2026)

### Key Findings

**ASTRIDE Framework:** A new automated threat modeling platform extends classical STRIDE with AI-specific attacks (instruction manipulation, unsafe tool invocation, reasoning subversion). Uses vision-language models to analyze agent architecture diagrams.

**Prompt Injection Crisis:** OWASP LLM01:2025 lists prompt injection as the #1 vulnerability. Models cannot distinguish instructions from data. Current defenses (rate limiting, content filters) only slow attacks—89% success on GPT-4o, 78% on Claude 3.5 Sonnet with sufficient attempts.

**OWASP Top 10 LLM (2025):** Covers prompt injection, sensitive disclosure, supply chain, data/model poisoning, improper output handling, excessive agency, system prompt leakage, vector weaknesses, misinformation, unbounded consumption.

**Supply Chain Attacks:** Malicious AI skills in marketplaces pose critical risk. ClawHub: 341 of 2,857 skills were malicious. LiteLLM: supply chain attack compromised PyPI package (97M monthly downloads, used by Stripe/Netflix/Google). Malicious skills use Base64 encoding and trusted platforms to hide malware.

### Critical for Your Codebase

Risk in skill files: validation, signing, sandboxing required. Malicious skills can execute arbitrary commands through documented workflows.

## Agent 91: MEASURING NARRATIVE EFFECTIVENESS: BEYOND PAGEVIEWS

*Source: ada8081d08a6eb243.output*

I'll search for comprehensive information on measuring narrative and content effectiveness beyond standard metrics.
## MEASURING NARRATIVE EFFECTIVENESS: BEYOND PAGEVIEWS

**Core Principle:** Success = Behavior Change, Not Just Reach

Narrative effectiveness moves beyond vanity metrics to measure *whether content actually works*—moving readers, changing perceptions, driving decisions.

### Key Metrics Framework

**Scroll Depth (Engagement):** Measures how far users scroll (0-100%), revealing if your story unfolds compellingly enough to keep readers engaged past skimmable sections. High scroll indicates narrative resonance; users stay for the full arc.

**Time on Page (Resonance):** Extended time signals meaningful interaction—readers aren't just landing, they're absorbing. Combine with scroll depth: deep scrolling + short time = skimming; deep scrolling + long time = genuine engagement.

**Micro-Conversions (Narrative Momentum):** Track smaller story completion signals—email signups, PDF downloads, share buttons clicked—showing narrative persuasion before final conversion.

**Conversion Rate (Story Completion):** The ultimate metric: did the narrative move users to action? Macro-conversions (purchases, signups) prove your story's persuasive power.

### Qualitative Signals

- **Sentiment Analysis:** Social listening tools measure emotional response—does your narrative evoke the intended feeling?
- **Comments & Shares:** Organic amplification reveals if readers felt compelled to spread your story.
- **A/B Testing:** Compare narrative versions (structure, tone, framing) to see which story architecture performs better.

### Integration Strategy

Combine quantitative (scroll, time, conversion) with qualitative (feedback, sentiment, shares). Output metrics show *if* content was received; outcome metrics show *if it changed minds*—the true test of narrative effectiveness.

---

**Sources:**
- [Measuring What Matters: Digital Content Effectiveness Metrics](https://marketingcourse.org/measuring-what-matters-a-guide-to-digital-content-effectiveness-metrics/)
- [Scroll Depth as Engagement Metric](https://freedatalytics.com/wiki/scroll-depth/)
- [Measuring Narrative Engagement at Staffbase](https://staffbase.com/blog/measuring-narrative-engagement)
- [Conversion Rate KPIs for Copywriters](https://copyhackers.com/top-10-kpis-for-conversion-copywriters/)
- [Content Engagement Metrics Guide](https://www.klipfolio.com/resources/kpi-examples/digital-marketing/content-engagement)

## Agent 92: Cross-Cultural Web Narrative: Research Summary

*Source: ade1f1ce0f37423b6.output*

I'll search for information about how web narratives change across cultures, focusing on the frameworks and principles you mentioned.
## Cross-Cultural Web Narrative: Research Summary

**Frameworks & Dimensions:**
Web narrative adaptation pivots on [Hofstede's cultural dimensions](https://medium.com/design-bootcamp/hofstade-model-for-ux-designers-5b9ed3105f90) (power distance, individualism/collectivism, uncertainty avoidance) and [high-context vs. low-context communication](https://www.tingalls.com/tips/2014/05/is-your-website-design-aimed-at-high-context-or-low-context-customers/). These frameworks reveal that effective localization requires fundamental narrative shifts, not just translation.

**High-Context vs. Low-Context:**
[Low-context cultures](https://designwebkit.com/web-and-trends/context-culture-in-web-design/) demand explicit, linear, detailed information with clear navigation. [High-context cultures](https://www.acclaro.com/blog/localizing-websites-for-high-context-cultures/) prefer implied meaning, visual richness, and relational context. Low-context sites minimize animation; high-context sites embrace detailed design and imagery.

**Messaging Dimensions:**
[Individualist messaging](http://mastertcloc.unistra.fr/2017/02/27/individualism-collectivism-localization/) emphasizes personal achievement and direct calls-to-action (single faces gazing at users). [Collectivist messaging](https://www.cb.cityu.edu.hk/people-and-research/research-insights/information-systems/website-localization-strategies-promote-global-e-commerce-the-moderating-role) foregrounds group imagery, company history, and social proof ("most popular" categories resonate strongly). Individualist cultures prefer text/email; collectivist cultures prefer face-to-face visual communication.

**Color Symbolism:**
[Universally positive: Blue](https://www.adobe.com/creativecloud/design/discover/color-meaning.html). [Culturally variable: White](https://cieden.com/book/sub-atomic/color/color-symbolism-and-meanings) (purity West; mourning East), [Red](https://design4users.com/design-for-diversity-of-cultures-perception-of-colors/) (passion West; luck/joy East), [Black](https://webdesignerdepot.com/color-and-cultural-design-considerations/) (death West; wisdom East).

**What's Universal:** [Clear, accessible design](https://www.nngroup.com/articles/crosscultural-design/), narrative authenticity, and avoiding jargon. [Narrative framing matters](https://www.tekla-szymanski.com/cross-cultural-design/)—opportunity-focused messaging outperforms deficit framing across cultures.

Sources: Adobe, Acclaro, NNGroup, Smashing Magazine, academic research across Springer & Oxford.

## Agent 93: Next.js Security Briefing

*Source: ade294ba2907c8007.output*

I'll search for the latest Next.js security concerns to identify framework-specific risks.
## Next.js Security Briefing

**Critical Framework-Specific Risks (2026):**

### RSC/Server Component Vulnerabilities
- **CVE-2025-55182/CVE-2026-66478 (CVSS 10.0 - RCE)**: Malicious HTTP requests to App Router endpoints exploit Flight Protocol deserialization, enabling unauthenticated remote code execution. Active exploitation observed.
- **CVE-2025-55184 (CVSS 7.5 - DoS)**: Specially-crafted requests cause server CPU exhaustion and process hangs.
- **CVE-2025-55183 (CVSS 5.3 - Source Exposure)**: HTTP requests leak source code when Server Components return stringifiable data from user input.

### Server Actions Threats
- **No Built-in Auth**: Server Actions are public HTTP endpoints; `"use server"` doesn't enforce authentication.
- **Closure Data Leakage**: Sensitive data captured in Server Action closures serializes to client (encrypted but increases attack surface).
- **Type Safety Gap**: TypeScript types strip at runtime; pass `{"userId": {"$ne": null}}` to bypass validation.

### Middleware Bypass
- **CVE-9.1 Severity**: `x-middleware-subrequest` header bypasses all middleware, skipping authentication checks.

**Sentinel Agent must warn:**
1. Update React/Next.js immediately (Dec 2025+ patches critical)
2. Validate ALL Server Action inputs (don't trust types)
3. Never pass secrets to Server Component props
4. Treat Server Actions as public API endpoints requiring explicit auth
5. Audit RSC data flows for PII/credential exposure

**Sources:**
- [React Critical RSC Vulnerability](https://react.dev/blog/2025/12/03/critical-security-vulnerability-in-react-server-components)
- [Next.js Server Actions Security](https://makerkit.dev/blog/tutorials/secure-nextjs-server-actions)
- [CVE-2025-55182 RCE Details](https://www.offsec.com/blog/cve-2025-55182/)
- [Akamai CVE-2026-23864 DoS](https://www.akamai.com/blog/security-research/cve-2026-23864-react-nextjs-denial-of-service)

## Agent 94: adeba2eb31f3707fb

*Source: adeba2eb31f3707fb.output*

I'll read the PDF directly using the Read tool.
The PDF is fully read (17 pages, all content visible). Here is the complete extraction across all seven requested dimensions.

---

**Duarte Resonate Methodology — Digital Product Adaptation: Complete Extraction**

---

**1. The Digital Sparkline — Scroll Depth as Emotional Oscillation**

The Sparkline translates from time (keynote) to space (scroll). The Y-axis of the browser is the timeline; the emotional X-axis oscillates between two poles:

- **Left/negative pole: Status Quo (Pain/Agitation)** — validates current friction, broken workflows, lost revenue
- **Right/positive pole: Visionary Future (Solution/New Bliss)** — reveals the interface, the automated outcome, the success state

The mechanics: the user's attention is a fluctuating resource requiring constant replenishment. A page that stays at either pole "flatlines" — either a brag sheet (stuck in Visionary Future) or a grievance list inducing apathy (stuck in Status Quo). The scroll must create a sinusoidal rhythm of tension and release. The viewport is a cinematic frame; the scroll action is the cut; a 5000px landing page is a film strip.

Pixel-level sparkline sequence from the document's example (project management tool):
- 0–600px: Status Quo — "Your team is drowning in email threads" (chaotic notification montage)
- 600–1200px: Visionary Future — "Reclaim your sanity with a single source of truth" (clean board interface)
- 1200–1800px: Status Quo — "But deadlines keep slipping" (red alert icon)
- 1800–2400px: Visionary Future — "Predict bottlenecks before they happen" (Gantt chart, resolved timeline)

---

**2. Section-by-Section Mapping**

**Hero (Inciting Incident):** Must encapsulate the entire sparkline in microcosm — the first beat.
- Hook: acknowledge Status Quo (headline addresses the need, e.g., "Stop Wasting Time on Spreadsheets")
- Promise: flash Visionary Future immediately (sub-headline, e.g., "Automate Your Financial Close in Seconds")
- Visual strategy: background image/video depicts the result (New Bliss); headline addresses the need (Status Quo) — juxtaposition creates immediate narrative tension

**Agitation Layer (Grounding in Reality):** Pulls the user back to Status Quo after the hero. Validates that the interface understands the user's struggle.
- UI patterns: "Old Way vs. New Way" comparison tables, "Problem" grids highlighting specific friction points
- Duarte's Rule: be specific about pain — "Data silos are costing you 20% revenue" — to create tension required for subsequent release
- This is where the sparkline dips to the negative pole

**Solution Layer (Crossing the Threshold):** The upswing — release of agitation tension.
- Narrative function: the software ("Magical Gift") introduced as mechanism of change
- UI patterns: high-fidelity screenshots, interactive demos, scrollytelling animations showing workflows completing
- Emotional shift: visual language transitions from chaotic/complex to clean/organized/serene — the user physically feels the relief as elements reorganize into a coherent dashboard

**STAR Moment (Something They'll Always Remember):** Placed at 60–70% scroll depth, after problem and solution are firmly established.
- In digital product context: translates to an Interactive Simulator or ROI Calculator
- Function: personalizes the Visionary Future — user inputs their own data, interface returns "You could save $14,000/year" — transforms generic promise into specific, tangible outcome

**CTA:** Terminal section at page bottom; functions as the threshold-crossing invitation after the full sparkline has run.

---

**3. The Mentor Archetype in UX — Brand as Yoda, User as Luke**

The core inversion: the most common UX failure is the "Company as Hero" fallacy — "We are the leading solution," "Our features are the best." Duarte's methodology, drawing from Campbell's Monomyth, explicitly rejects this. **The User is Luke. The Interface is Yoda.**

This dictates the grammatical structure of every button, empty state, and error message.

Specific copy implications:

| Context | System Voice (Villain) | Mentor Voice (Guide) |
|---|---|---|
| Empty State | "No tasks found." | "You're all caught up! Add a new task to stay productive." |
| Critical Error | "Oops! Something went wrong..." | "We are experiencing a connection issue. Please refresh the page." |
| Input Validation | "INVALID INPUT" | "Please enter a valid email address (e.g., name@example.com)." |
| 404 | "404 Error: Page not found." | "We couldn't find that page. Let's get you back Home." |
| Success State | "Form submitted." | "Your application has been sent. We will review it shortly." |

Mentor's Code (Do's): Empower don't command ("Add your details to unlock your dashboard" not "You must fill out this field"); Anticipate the struggle with Smart Defaults and Contextual Tooltips before the user fails; Celebrate wins ("Great job, your campaign is live!" not "Campaign status: Active").

Mentor's Taboos (Don'ts): Never blame the Hero in error messages; avoid robot-speak (translate "Database connection error 504" into a narrative setback that can be overcome).

Feature copy rule: describe empowerment, not the tool. "AI-Powered Analytics Engine" becomes "See your future revenue with AI prediction."

---

**4. Three-Act Structure for Scroll — 0–20% / 20–75% / 75–100%**

The document maps user lifecycle (not just page scroll) to The Departure / Initiation / Return:

**Act 1 — The Departure (0–20%, Onboarding and Activation):**
- Narrative goal: Cross the Threshold and achieve initial success
- User exists in Ordinary World (Status Quo), encounters the Call to Adventure (marketing site / sign-up)
- Inciting Incident: pain awareness drives them to the product
- Crossing the Threshold: the sign-up form — the point of no return
- "Aha!" Moment: Mentor (UI) must demonstrate the Magical Gift quickly (Time to First Value / TTFV)
- Design strategy: reduce friction, Progressive Disclosure, get to the "Aha!" moment before asking for 20 fields

**Act 2 — The Initiation (20–75%, Adoption and Friction):**
- Narrative goal: Overcome conflict, internalize the New Bliss
- The "Messy Middle" — the Road of Trials, habit formation, integration
- The Trough of Sorrow: natural dip in enthusiasm after Act 1 hype
- Necessary friction (learning curve) is the Ordeal the Hero must face to gain competence; unnecessary friction is fatal
- Mentor's Intervention: gamification (progress bars, checklists, milestones = "rising action"); contextual guidance at the moment of greatest need ("It looks like you're stuck. Try this template.")

**Act 3 — The Return (75–100%, Retention and Advocacy):**
- Narrative goal: Return with the Elixir
- The Climax: user achieves primary goal (sends first invoice, launches first campaign)
- New Bliss (Resolution): interface confirms success, establishes new Status Quo where the user is powerful and efficient — visually distinct, a moment of calm and order
- The Elixir (Advocacy): Hero returns to their Ordinary World and shares the tool — the Referral loop. The Hero becomes the Mentor for others. Narrative cycle completes; a new one begins.

---

**5. The Big Idea Formula — Subject + Verb + Outcome with Stakes**

Three mandatory criteria:
1. It must articulate a unique Point of View (cannot be generic, must take a stand)
2. It must convey what is at stake (consequences of inaction vs. reward of action)
3. It must be a complete sentence: **Subject + Verb + Outcome**

Weak: "We provide cloud-based accounting software." — feature-focused, passive, describes the tool not the transformation.

Resonant Big Idea: "Your business will collapse without real-time cash flow visibility, but our platform grants you financial omniscience." — high stakes, clear outcome, emotional resonance, positions user as potential victim of chaos or master of their destiny.

The Feature-Benefit-Emotion Ladder climbs to the Big Idea:
- Rung 1 Feature: "Automated receipt scanning"
- Rung 2 Functional Benefit: "Save 2 hours of data entry per week"
- Rung 3 Emotional Benefit: "Peace of mind knowing you are audit-proof"
- Rung 4 Big Idea (Transformation): "Become the strategic leader who drives profit, not just the admin who tracks costs"

Once defined, the Big Idea operates as North Star: H1 headline on the homepage; filter for "Success State" messages inside the product ("Strategic insight generated" not "Report saved"); razor for feature prioritization — any feature that does not support the Transformation is cut or deprioritized.

---

**6. Snow Fall vs. SaaS Scrollytelling — Linear Plot vs. Dialectic Argument**

The NYT "Snow Fall" piece uses scroll depth to advance a **linear plot** (chronology of an avalanche — event follows event in time).

SaaS scrollytelling uses scroll depth to advance a **dialectic argument**:
- Thesis: Your Problem
- Antithesis: Our Solution
- Synthesis: Your Success

The scroll is not storytelling a sequence of events — it is prosecuting an argument. Each scroll beat either deepens the problem or reveals a dimension of the solution. The oscillation keeps the argument alive by constantly renewing the problem/solution dynamic, preventing the flatline of a feature dump.

---

**7. Oscillation Frequency Rules — Fast vs. Slow Based on Product Complexity**

The document gives explicit pixel-based rules:

**Fast oscillation (simple, low-stakes products, impulse conversions):**
- Problem headline immediately followed by solution screenshot
- Then problem statistic followed by solution benefit
- Cycle completes in ~600px increments
- Creates fast-paced, energetic rhythm
- Suitable for: B2C, consumer apps, e-commerce

**Slow oscillation (complex B2B enterprise software):**
- ~800px establishing the gravity of the Status Quo (the "Messy Middle" of legacy systems)
- Then ~1000px deep dive into the Visionary Future
- Longer immersion at each pole required because the audience needs time to internalize the stakes before the release lands with weight
- Suitable for: enterprise SaaS, high-ACV products with long consideration cycles

The governing principle: oscillation frequency must match the cognitive effort required to understand and trust the product. Faster audience sophistication and lower product complexity = higher frequency. Deeper complexity or skepticism = longer dwell at each pole before transitioning.

---

**Sparkline Mechanics Summary**

The Digital Sparkline is a spatial waveform: scroll depth (Y-axis of browser) mapped against emotional state (X-axis: Status Quo negative / Visionary Future positive). Every section of a page is a beat on this waveform. The browser viewport is the cinematic frame; the scroll event is the editorial cut. The designer's job is to be the editor of that timeline — never letting the waveform flatline, always ensuring each dip into pain is followed by a corresponding rise into possibility, with oscillation frequency calibrated to product complexity and audience sophistication.

---

## Agent 95: Accessibility Guidelines for Scroll-Driven Animations - Research Summary

*Source: adf368c3678d3a28d.output*

I'll search for current accessibility guidelines on scroll-driven animations and WCAG updates.
## Accessibility Guidelines for Scroll-Driven Animations - Research Summary

**Current Standards (WCAG 2.2):**
No new scroll-animation-specific guidelines beyond WCAG 2.2. Core requirements remain:
- **2.2.2 (Pause/Stop/Hide)**: Auto-playing animations lasting 5+ seconds need user controls
- **2.3.3 (Animation from Interactions - AAA)**: Motion from user interactions must be disableable unless essential

**Best Practices 2026:**
- **prefers-reduced-motion is mandatory**: Always implement CSS media query to detect reduced-motion preference and disable parallax/vestibular-triggering animations
- **Vestibular considerations**: Parallax scrolling and large-element transforms cause dizziness/nausea for users with vestibular disorders—replace with opacity transitions instead
- **No auto-play**: Disable auto-playing videos and motion-based content
- **Duration limits**: Keep animations under 5 seconds; provide explicit pause controls for longer content

**Key Resources:**
- [WCAG 2.2 Animation from Interactions](https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG22/Understanding/animation-from-interactions.html)
- [MDN prefers-reduced-motion](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/CSS/Reference/At-rules/@media/prefers-reduced-motion)
- [Web.dev Prefers-Reduced-Motion Article](https://web.dev/articles/prefers-reduced-motion)
- [CSS-Tricks Animation Accessibility](https://css-tricks.com/accessible-web-animation-the-wcag-on-animation-explained/)

No major updates beyond WCAG 2.2—implementation focus remains on respecting user motion preferences and removing vestibular triggers.

## Agent 96: Dashboard and Admin Panel Narrative Patterns Report

*Source: adf7927ea276914b1.output*

I'll search for dashboard and admin panel narrative patterns with focus on UX writing, copy patterns, and microcopy strategies.
## Dashboard and Admin Panel Narrative Patterns Report

**Core Finding:** Narrative isn't reserved for storytelling products. Data-heavy dashboards use microcopy, empty states, and guided tours to create sequential user journeys that guide action and build understanding.

### Microcopy as Storytelling

Small, contextual text (button labels, tooltips, form fields) acts as "teachers and translators" in admin panels. Rather than passive documentation, effective microcopy uses action-oriented language ("Download Report" not "Click Here") and leads with user benefits. The [3 I's framework](https://www.nngroup.com/articles/3-is-of-microcopy/) — Inform, Influence, Interact — applies narrative tension by showing what's possible, why it matters, and how to act.

### Empty States as Narrative Hooks

[Empty states aren't blank](https://www.chameleon.io/blog/how-to-use-empty-states-for-better-onboarding/) — they're staged moments that welcome users into data structures. Best-in-class dashboards (e.g., Trello) populate empty states with sample data and explanatory microcopy, turning a void into a guided story arc: "Here's what's possible → Here's how to fill this space → Here's what happens next."

### Guided Tours as Narrative Structure

[Guided tours](https://www.patternfly.org/ux-writing/guided-tours/) for enterprise software aren't mechanical walkthroughs — they're sequential narratives that connect user goals to product capabilities. Red Hat's pattern library emphasizes conversational copy that addresses pain points, not just features.

### B2B Dashboard Specifics

Enterprise onboarding platforms ([Rocketlane](https://www.rocketlane.com/blogs/customer-onboarding-tools), Gainsight) structure dashboards as "status + next action" narratives — teams see what's blocked and why, not raw data. [This reduces cognitive load](https://auth0.com/blog/user-onboarding-strategies-b2b-saas/) by embedding narrative guidance directly into metrics and workflows.

**Key Takeaway:** Data labels, empty states, and onboarding flows are narrative opportunities. Each microcopy element answers "Why does this exist?" before "How do I use it?"

---

**Key Sources:**
- [NN/G: Microcopy UX](https://www.nngroup.com/articles/ux-copy-sizes/) | [UserPilot](https://userpilot.com/blog/microcopy-ux/) | [Smashing Mag](https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2024/06/how-improve-microcopy-ux-writing-tips-non-ux-writers/) | [Empty States Onboarding](https://www.useronboard.com/onboarding-ux-patterns/empty-states/) | [Chameleon](https://www.chameleon.io/blog/how-to-use-empty-states-for-better-onboarding/)

## Agent 97: Audience Analysis Frameworks for Web Copy: Comparative Report

*Source: ae3872036b63cc159.output*

I'll search for audience analysis frameworks for web copy to give you a comprehensive overview.
## Audience Analysis Frameworks for Web Copy: Comparative Report

**Four Major Approaches:**

1. **Buyer Personas** - The "who" (demographics, behaviors, personalities). Best for understanding audience segments and channel preferences. Limited by their static nature; they don't account for context variation or why people make decisions.

2. **Jobs to Be Done (JTBD)** - The "why" and "what" (functional, emotional, social progress). Ideal for copywriting because it reveals the specific circumstance and desired outcome. Use customer language directly in copy to mirror their struggle. Powerful for narrative-driven content and sales pages.

3. **Empathy Maps** - The "how they think/feel" (says, thinks, feels, does). Best deployed early in design after research but before requirements. Reveals psychological barriers and emotional needs. Useful for synthesizing research and establishing team alignment.

4. **Awareness Levels** - Implicit in copy strategy; targets attention (unaware), interest (aware of problem), desire (aware of solution), and action (ready to buy). Applied iteratively across your narrative.

**Which Framework for What:**

- **Complex B2B or strategic features**: Start with empathy maps to understand emotions, then layer JTBD for narrative copy
- **Product-market messaging**: Combine personas (segment targeting) + JTBD (value articulation)
- **Landing pages/sales copy**: JTBD is superior; craft narrative around specific job, then speak to emotional/functional outcomes
- **Understanding diversity**: Use personas; ignore for copy strategy alone
- **Team alignment before writing**: Empathy maps first

**Pre-Writing Research Process:**
1. Conduct interviews revealing the job they're trying to do and the circumstance triggering it
2. Create empathy maps from research observations
3. Build JTBD narratives using customer language captured
4. Draft copy mirroring the customer's struggle and solution

**Best Practice:** Use JTBD as primary copywriting guide, personas for audience segmentation, empathy maps for team understanding.

### Sources
- [Persona vs JTBD Comparison](https://blog.marketmuse.com/buyer-personas-vs-jobs-to-be-done/)
- [JTBD for Copywriting](https://copyhackers.com/2014/11/jobs-to-be-done-copywriting/)
- [Empathy Mapping Guide](https://www.nngroup.com/articles/empathy-mapping/)
- [Applied JTBD to Web Copy](https://www.brookslockett.com/post/applying-jobs-to-be-done-to-website-copy-examples)
- [Audience Analysis Foundation](https://jakegroup.com/blog/how-to-conduct-an-audience-analysis-for-your-website/)

## Mission 17 — Attention Economics & Cognitive Bandwidth

**Findings:**

The 50 bits/sec figure has been effectively superseded. A December 2024 Caltech paper ("The unbearable slowness of being: Why do we live at 10 bits/s?" — Zheng & Meister, published in *Neuron*) is now the landmark reference. It revises the model downward to **10 bits/sec conscious throughput**, while sensory input runs at ~1 billion bits/sec. The key insight is not just the number — it is that the brain processes one sequential "path" of thought at a time, which means multitasking UI (multiple competing animations, choices, messages) is actively hostile to comprehension.

In the 2026 design mainstream this is translating into: mobile-first performance stripping, AI-powered personalization to reduce the decision surface, and typography-as-hierarchy as an attention-routing mechanism. The framing has shifted from "short attention span" to "the bottleneck is the brain, not the interface" — meaning the right response is sequencing and progressive disclosure, not simply brevity.

**Sources:**
- [The unbearable slowness of being (Caltech/Neuron, 2024)](https://meisterlab.caltech.edu/documents/30100/Zheng_2024_The_unbearable_slowness_of_being-_Why_do_we_live_at_10_bitss.pdf)
- [Caltech Magazine — The Remarkably Slow Speed of Thought](https://magazine.caltech.edu/post/speed-of-thought-meister-zheng)
- [SciTechDaily — 10 Bits Per Second Caltech Study](https://scitechdaily.com/caltech-scientists-discover-the-surprising-speed-limit-of-human-thought-just-10-bits-per-second/)
- [arxiv preprint](https://arxiv.org/html/2408.10234v2)
- [Webflow — Web Design Trends 2026](https://webflow.com/blog/web-design-trends-2026)
- [uxtigers — Think-Time UX](https://www.uxtigers.com/post/think-time-ux)

**Relevance to web-god:** web-god's scrollytelling and progressive disclosure architecture is directly validated by the 10 bits/sec model. Sequential, paced reveal is not an aesthetic choice — it is cognitively mandatory. The implication for web-god's agents is that *information density per scroll beat* matters more than total content quantity. The old 50 bits/sec figure should be replaced with the Meister/Zheng figure in any internal docs, and scroll pacing guidance should cite it.

**Action:** Update web-god's scrollytelling reference docs to cite the 10 bits/sec Caltech 2024 research instead of (or alongside) 50 bits/sec. Audit whether scroll beat pacing in the scrollytelling skill aligns with sequential, one-idea-at-a-time exposure. Priority: **P1**, Effort: **S**

## Mission 19 — Scroll Behavior UX Research

**Findings:**

The **Contentsquare 2026 Digital Experience Benchmark** (built from 99 billion sessions, 6,500+ websites) is the most authoritative current data source. Key numbers:

- Overall engagement fell **-10% year-on-year**
- Scroll rate: **50.5% desktop, 45.2% mobile** (desktop leads by 5pp)
- Users spending less time on-site, viewing fewer pages, scrolling less
- Countervailing signal: visitors arrive more informed (AI-influenced traffic), so lower scroll does not equal lower intent

From other sources: average decision window before engagement or exit is **1.7 seconds**; average attention span measured at 8.25 seconds (though this figure is disputed in methodology). Concrete scroll depth improvement: restructuring longform with subheadings + visuals every 300 words produced a **50% increase in average scroll depth** in a documented case study.

Scrollytelling-specific completion rate data is not yet published in academic or industry benchmark form for 2025-2026 — Maglr and similar platforms have examples but no published aggregate metrics.

**Sources:**
- [Contentsquare — 2026 Digital Experience Benchmark](https://contentsquare.com/guides/digital-experience-benchmark/)

**Relevance to web-god:** The -10% engagement decline plus the 1.7-second decision window puts enormous pressure on the first scroll beat. web-god's scrollytelling pipeline needs a "hook density" concept — front-loading the most compelling signal within the first viewport. The desktop/mobile gap (50.5% vs 45.2%) also matters for scroll animation defaults. The "subheadings + visuals every 300 words = +50% scroll depth" finding should directly inform web-god's content structure recommendations.

**Action:** Add a "hook density" first-viewport principle to web-god's scrollytelling skill. Add the 300-word visual break cadence as a structural guideline. Include Contentsquare 2026 data as a living benchmark reference. Priority: **P1**, Effort: **M**

## Mission 20 — Accessibility & Scroll Animations

**Findings:**

**WCAG 2.2** (October 2023) is the current legal standard under ADA, Section 508, and the European Accessibility Act (EAA, mandatory by 2025). The key criterion for scroll animation is **WCAG 2.3.3 (Animation from Interactions, Level AAA)** — parallax and scroll-triggered animations must be disableable unless essential. WCAG **2.2.2 (Pause, Stop, Hide)** requires on-page controls for auto-playing motion.

**WCAG 3.0** is in active Working Draft as of March 2026 (most recent draft published March 3, 2026). W3C published 174 new outcomes in the 3.0 draft. Candidate recommendation is not expected until Q4 2027; full recommendation not before 2028. The 3.0 draft shifts to an outcomes/app-level testing model but no specific animation criterion changes have been surfaced in current drafts.

Best practice in 2026 goes beyond a single CSS media query. The layered model is:
1. `prefers-reduced-motion: reduce` — CSS fallback (supported in all major browsers)
2. On-page pause/stop controls — required for auto-playing content (2.2.2 AA)
3. Alternative non-motion versions of key animations — not just "turn off" but "substitute"
4. Avoid animations exceeding 5 seconds; no content flashing >3x per second
5. Vestibular triggers to specifically avoid: scaling, parallax, rotation, wave effects. Safe substitutes: fade, dissolve, color-change

The Pope Tech December 2025 guide is the most current practitioner-level resource.

**Sources:**
- [W3C — WCAG 3 Working Draft March 2026](https://www.w3.org/WAI/news/2026-03-03/wcag3/)
- [W3C — WCAG 2.2](https://www.w3.org/TR/WCAG22/)
- [W3C — Understanding 2.3.3 Animation from Interactions](https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/animation-from-interactions.html)
- [Pope Tech — Accessible Animation Guide (Dec 2025)](https://blog.pope.tech/2025/12/08/design-accessible-animation-and-movement/)
- [MDN — prefers-reduced-motion](https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/CSS/Reference/At-rules/@media/prefers-reduced-motion)
- [motion.dev — React Accessibility Guide](https://motion.dev/docs/react-accessibility)
- [AllAccessible — WCAG 2.2 Compliance Guide 2025](https://www.allaccessible.org/blog/wcag-22-complete-guide-2025)
- [Deque — WCAG 3.0 174 New Outcomes](https://www.deque.com/blog/w3c-unveils-174-new-outcomes-for-wcag-3-0/)
- [AbilityNet — WCAG 3.0 2026 Overview](https://abilitynet.org.uk/resources/digital-accessibility/what-expect-wcag-30-web-content-accessibility-guidelines)
- [browserux.com — CSS prefers-reduced-motion](https://browserux.com/blog/articles/css-prefers-reduced-motion-accessibility.html)

**Relevance to web-god:** web-god likely implements `prefers-reduced-motion` but the 2026 standard expects the full layered model. Specifically: (1) WCAG 2.3.3 is Level AAA — if web-god claims AAA compliance for scroll animations, paralax must be disableable; (2) on-page pause controls are AA-required (2.2.2), not optional; (3) the substitution pattern (fade instead of scale/parallax) is more accessible than full motion removal and should be the default alternative. WCAG 3.0 won't be binding until 2028+ so no urgent legal risk, but early adoption of 3.0 outcomes-based framing is good positioning.

**Action:** Audit web-god's scroll animation implementation for WCAG 2.2.2 on-page pause controls (AA, required now) and WCAG 2.3.3 disableability (AAA, best practice). Add the substitution pattern (fade/dissolve fallback vs. full removal) to the a11y layer of the scrollytelling skill. Monitor WCAG 3.0 Working Draft — next milestone is April 2026 projected timeline. Priority: **P1**, Effort: **M**

## Mission 29 — Next.js Security Patterns

**Findings:**

Next.js has had a severe sequence of vulnerabilities that fundamentally change how web-god should advise on its use:

**CVE-2025-29927 (CVSS 9.1) — Middleware Authorization Bypass.** An attacker sends the `x-middleware-subrequest` header to bypass all middleware logic without credentials. Affected: Next.js 11.1.4–15.2.2. Fixed in 15.2.3+. The core lesson: middleware is NOT a security boundary and must never be the only auth gate.

**CVE-2025-55182 (CVSS 10.0) — React2Shell RCE.** Remote code execution in React Server Components via crafted HTTP requests. Disclosed Dec 3, 2025.

**CVE-2025-55183 — RSC Source Code Exposure.** A crafted HTTP request to a Server Action returns the compiled source of other Server Functions, exposing business logic and any secrets hardcoded in source (not in env vars). Observed attackers then used TruffleHog/Gitleaks to harvest API keys, OpenAI tokens, cloud credentials.

**CVE-2025-55184 / CVE-2025-67779 — DoS via infinite loop** in Server Function endpoints (CVSS 7.5).

**CVE-2026-27978 — Server Actions CSRF bypass.** `origin: null` (sandboxed iframes, local HTML files) was treated as a missing origin rather than cross-origin, bypassing CSRF validation. Fixed in Next.js 16.1.7+.

**Current architectural rules (2026):**
- Auth checks must live in Route Handlers, Server Actions, and DAL — not just middleware.
- Secrets must be in env vars exclusively; source code exposure vulns make hardcoding a critical risk.
- Passkeys (WebAuthn/FIDO2) are now the recommended default for new apps — all major platforms support natively.
- Every Server Action needs explicit ownership/IDOR checks and server-side Zod validation.

**Sources:**
- [Next.js Security Best Practices 2026 — Authgear](https://www.authgear.com/post/nextjs-security-best-practices)
- [Next.js Data Security Guide](https://nextjs.org/docs/app/guides/data-security)
- [Next.js Security Update Dec 11 2025](https://nextjs.org/blog/security-update-2025-12-11)
- [Critical RSC Vulnerability — React blog](https://react.dev/blog/2025/12/03/critical-security-vulnerability-in-react-server-components)
- [DoS and Source Code Exposure in RSC — React blog](https://react.dev/blog/2025/12/11/denial-of-service-and-source-code-exposure-in-react-server-components)
- [CVE-2026-27978 — Null origin CSRF bypass](https://advisories.gitlab.com/pkg/npm/next/CVE-2026-27978/)
- [React2Shell Microsoft Security Blog](https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/security/blog/2025/12/15/defending-against-the-cve-2025-55182-react2shell-vulnerability-in-react-server-components/)
- [CVE-2025-29927 breakdown — Strobes](https://strobes.co/blog/understanding-next-js-vulnerability/)
- [Next.js Security Testing Guide — DeepStrike](https://deepstrike.io/blog/nextjs-security-testing-bug-bounty-guide)

**Relevance to web-god:** The `threat-modeler.md` agent and `security-principles.md` reference are missing Next.js-specific threat categories. The current agent only covers generic OWASP patterns. RSC source-code exposure, Server Action CSRF, and the "middleware is not a security boundary" rule are all absent.

**Action:** Update `agents/security/threat-modeler.md` to add a Next.js-specific section covering: (1) middleware-as-not-a-boundary rule, (2) Server Action auth/IDOR checklist, (3) RSC source code exposure via hardcoded secrets, (4) CSRF on Server Actions / null-origin bypass, (5) passkeys as default recommendation. Also add a "Next.js CVE Watch" note to `skills/security/references/security-principles.md`. Priority: **P0**, Effort: **S**

## Mission 30 — npm Supply Chain Security

**Findings:**

The npm supply chain threat reached a new severity level in 2026:

**The Axios Compromise (March 31, 2026 — today).** A threat actor compromised an axios maintainer npm account and published malicious versions v1.14.1 and v0.30.4 containing a RAT. Axios has ~100M downloads/week and is present in ~80% of cloud environments. The absence of SLSA provenance attestation on the malicious versions was the key detection signal — legitimate releases are published via GitHub Actions OIDC-backed trusted publishing.

**Scale:** 454,648 malicious packages were published to npm in 2025. 65% of teams admit to bypassing or delaying fixes due to alert fatigue.

**Key defenses now standard:**
1. **Package provenance / SLSA attestations** — npm now supports provenance statements that cryptographically link a package to its source repo and build pipeline. Absence of provenance on a package that previously had it is a red flag.
2. **Trusted publishing** — publish via GitHub Actions OIDC rather than personal access tokens. Makes account compromise insufficient to publish.
3. **Socket.dev** — now scanning AI agent skills across ecosystems, provides real-time malware blocking. `alias npm="socket npm"` blocks installs with malicious behavior.
4. **`npm ci` over `npm install`** — strictly follows lockfile; safer for automated deployments.
5. **Lockfile integrity** — verify lockfile on every install, flag any unexpected changes in PRs.

**What `npm audit` cannot do:** It only flags known CVEs in the registry database. It cannot detect novel malware, behavior-based attacks, or compromised-but-not-yet-flagged packages like axios v1.14.1 at time of publish.

**Sources:**
- [npm Supply Chain Attacks 2026 — Bastion](https://bastion.tech/blog/npm-supply-chain-attacks-2026-saas-security-guide)
- [Axios NPM Distribution Compromised — Wiz](https://www.wiz.io/blog/axios-npm-compromised-in-supply-chain-attack)
- [Axios Supply Chain Attack 2026 — SOCRadar](https://socradar.io/blog/axios-npm-supply-chain-attack-2026-ciso-guide/)
- [npm Package Provenance — GitHub Blog](https://github.blog/security/supply-chain-security/introducing-npm-package-provenance/)
- [npm Provenance Docs](https://docs.npmjs.com/generating-provenance-statements/)
- [Protecting from Axios Compromise — BSWEN](https://docs.bswen.com/blog/2026-03-31-npm-supply-chain-protection/)
- [Securing NPM Supply Chain — The Candid Startup](https://www.thecandidstartup.org/2026/02/23/securing-npm-supply-chain.html)
- [pnpm newsroom npm supply chain security](https://pnpm.io/blog/2025/12/05/newsroom-npm-supply-chain-security)
- [npm Security Risks 2026 — CyberDesserts](https://blog.cyberdesserts.com/npm-security-vulnerabilities/)

**Relevance to web-god:** The `devops-deploy-planner.md` agent and `devops-principles.md` reference only mention `npm audit in CI, fails on critical/high` and `dependencies pinned in lockfile`. This is now dangerously insufficient — the Axios attack bypassed both controls entirely. The `threat-modeler.md` checklist under Dependencies is similarly thin.

**Action:** Update `agents/devops/deploy-planner.md` pipeline to add: (1) provenance attestation verification step in CI, (2) Socket.dev integration alongside `npm audit`, (3) lockfile change detection on PRs, (4) note that `npm audit` alone is insufficient for behavior-based attacks. Update `agents/security/threat-modeler.md` Dependencies section similarly. Priority: **P0**, Effort: **S**

## Mission 31 — Edge Computing Patterns

**Findings:**

The edge compute landscape has matured into a clear set of patterns as of 2026:

**Platform landscape:**
- **Cloudflare Workers** — most mature, 300+ PoPs, V8 isolates with sub-1ms cold start, complete ecosystem (Workers KV for global caching, Durable Objects for coordination, D1 for relational data, R2 for object storage). Best for Cloudflare-native stacks or apps that want full edge control.
- **Vercel Edge Functions** — tightly integrated with Next.js, optimized for frontend-centered apps. Vercel's Fluid Compute (bytecode caching + predictive instance warming) has reduced cold starts to near-imperceptible levels. Best for Next.js + Vercel deployments.
- **AWS CloudFront Functions** — third major player, enterprise-scale but more complex.

**Key patterns in 2026:**
1. **Edge middleware for auth/personalization** — run token validation, geo-routing, A/B testing at edge without hitting origin.
2. **Edge compatibility audit** — middleware must be reviewed for: Node.js API usage (not available at edge), npm package compatibility, CPU time under 25-50ms, and data access patterns.
3. **Edge-first data** — pair with D1/Turso/KV for truly edge-co-located data (see Mission 32).
4. **Phased migration** — migrating to edge is weeks not days; measure each change, identify runtime incompatibilities early.

**Vercel Fluid Compute benchmark context:** Vercel benchmarked Fluid Compute against Cloudflare Workers for server rendering, showing competitive cold start performance for Next.js apps on Vercel vs. raw Cloudflare Workers.

**Sources:**
- [Edge Computing 2026 Web Performance Architecture](https://www.digitalapplied.com/blog/edge-computing-2026-web-performance-architecture)
- [Cloudflare Workers Dev Guide 2026](https://www.digitalapplied.com/blog/edge-computing-cloudflare-workers-development-guide-2026)
- [Cloudflare vs Vercel vs Netlify Edge Performance 2026 — DEV](https://dev.to/dataformathub/cloudflare-vs-vercel-vs-netlify-the-truth-about-edge-performance-2026-50h0)
- [Vercel vs Cloudflare Edge Deep Dive — SparkCo](https://sparkco.ai/blog/vercel-vs-cloudflare-edge-deployment-deep-dive)
- [Fluid Compute Benchmark — Vercel](https://vercel.com/blog/fluid-compute-benchmark-results)
- [JS on the Edge Architecture — Makers Den](https://makersden.io/blog/js-on-edge-architect-ultra-low-latency-apps-w-vercel-cloudflare-workers)

- [Edge Computing with Cloudflare Workers 2026 — Calmops](https://calmops.com/cloud/edge-computing-cloudflare-workers-complete-guide-2026/)

**Relevance to web-god:** The `devops-deploy-planner.md` mentions "Vercel (Recommended for Next.js)" but has no edge function guidance, no Cloudflare Workers pattern, and no edge compatibility checklist. The frontend and backend agents also have no edge-awareness (e.g., choosing edge-compatible ORMs, avoiding Node.js-only APIs in middleware).

**Action:** Add an edge deployment section to `agents/devops/deploy-planner.md` covering: (1) Vercel Edge Functions vs. Cloudflare Workers decision matrix, (2) edge compatibility audit checklist for middleware, (3) Fluid Compute as the recommended Vercel mode for Next.js. Consider adding a dedicated `agents/devops/edge-architect.md` agent for Cloudflare-native stacks. Priority: **P1**, Effort: **M**

## Mission 32 — Database Trends for Web Apps

**Findings:**

The serverless Postgres and edge database markets have undergone major structural changes:

**Neon vs Supabase — clear differentiation by 2026:**
- **Neon** (acquired by Databricks for ~$1B in May 2025): True architectural serverless — compute and storage separated, scale-to-zero, instant provisioning. Over 80% of databases on Neon are now provisioned by AI agents automatically. Per-agent database branching makes it ideal for agentic workloads and Vercel/Next.js stacks. Teams pair it with Clerk or Auth.js.
- **Supabase** (acquired OrioleDB, pursuing decoupled storage architecture): Serverless-as-abstraction rather than architecture — traditional dedicated compute underneath, but Edge Functions and Storage are serverless. Winner for full-stack apps needing realtime features, integrated auth, and storage.

**Edge SQLite — production ready as of 2026:**
- **Cloudflare D1**: GA with global read replication. SQLite semantics, managed replication via Workers binding — developer never manages a file or connection string.
- **Turso / LibSQL**: Open-source SQLite fork with server mode, HTTP replication, embedded replicas. Embedded replicas deliver single-digit millisecond reads since data is local. Distributed SQLite becoming "the new standard" for read-heavy apps at the edge.
- **Performance**: D1 slightly higher latency than Turso embedded replicas (PoP replication hops), but both beat managed Postgres cross-region (30-80ms) for read workloads. 90%+ of web apps (blogs, content, e-commerce catalogs, dashboards) are read-heavy and benefit.

**Conversation shift:** The question is no longer "is serverless Postgres cool?" but "does it actually work at production scale under real load?" — and the answer is increasingly yes, with caveats around write-heavy workloads.

**PlanetScale** launched Postgres-compatible product, aggressively positioning against Neon on performance and reliability.

**Sources:**
- [Neon vs Supabase Benchmarks — DesignRevision](https://designrevision.com/blog/supabase-vs-neon)
- [Why I Switched from Supabase to Neon — Medium](https://medium.com/@cdcore/why-i-switched-from-supabase-to-neon-and-you-should-too-1fb6be07a043)
- [Supabase vs Neon 2026 — GetAutonoma](https://www.getautonoma.com/blog/supabase-vs-neon)
- [Neon Postgres Review 2026 — Medium](https://medium.com/@philmcc/neon-postgres-review-serverless-postgresql-that-actually-scales-to-zero-ee14d4e109ba)
- [Best Database Software for Startups 2026 — Makerkit](https://makerkit.dev/blog/tutorials/best-database-software-startups)
- [Post-PostgreSQL: SQLite on the Edge Production Ready — SitePoint](https://www.sitepoint.com/sqlite-edge-production-readiness-2026/)
- [SQLite Renaissance 2026 — DEV](https://dev.to/pockit_tools/the-sqlite-renaissance-why-the-worlds-most-deployed-database-is-taking-over-production-in-2026-3jcc)
- [Distributed SQLite: Turso and LibSQL 2026 — DEV](https://dev.to/dataformathub/distributed-sqlite-why-libsql-and-turso-are-the-new-standard-in-2026-58fk)
- [Turso vs Cloudflare D1 — BuildMVPFast](https://www.buildmvpfast.com/compare/turso-vs-cloudflare)
- [Cloudflare D1 Docs](https://developers.cloudflare.com/d1/)

**Relevance to web-god:** The `api-architect.md` agent has no database recommendation section — it leaves database choice entirely open. In 2026, the choice significantly affects architecture (Supabase = integrated BaaS stack; Neon = pure DB + separate auth; D1/Turso = edge-co-located). The agent also mentions BullMQ/Inngest for queues but no ORM guidance against a specific DB.

**Action:** Add a database selection decision tree to `agents/backend/api-architect.md`: (1) Vercel/Next.js + no realtime → Neon, (2) full-stack with realtime/auth/storage → Supabase, (3) Cloudflare Workers stack or read-heavy edge app → D1 or Turso. Add a note about Neon's agentic workload fit (instant provisioning, per-agent branching). Priority: **P1**, Effort: **S**

## Agent 101: Awwwards 2026 Digital Craftsmanship Report: Full Extract

---

## Awwwards 2026 Digital Craftsmanship Report: Full Extract

### 1. "Expressive Utility" — What It Is and How It Differs from the Component Era

**Component Era (2018–2023):** Driven by React component libraries and design systems (Tailwind, MUI, etc.). The web became modular, predictable, and visually homogenized. Efficiency was the primary metric; uniqueness was treated as technical debt. Static minimalism dominated. Flatland.

**Expressive Utility (2024–2026):** A philosophical counter-shift where "flash" (motion, 3D, physics) *performs* the function — storytelling, navigation, branding. The defining characteristics:
- Reintegration of "soul" — idiosyncratic, high-fidelity, physics-based interactions baked into the core functional experience
- "Neo-Brutalism" and "Spatial UI" replace flat design as dominant visual languages
- Dynamic complexity over static minimalism
- The interaction *is* the interface, not decoration on top of it
- Driven by a user response to "sameness" of the algorithmic web — demand for handcrafted, uniquely responsive experiences

The scorecard contrast is precise:

| Pillar | 2023 Standard | 2026 Standard | Key Tech |
|---|---|---|---|
| Storytelling | Linear Parallax & Static Reveals | Immersive Narratives & Interactive Comics | WebGL / Canvas |
| Kinetic Type | Static Web Fonts | Animated & Variable | Variable Fonts |
| Interaction | Simple Hover States | Magnetic & Natural | CSS Transform |
| UI Architecture | Flat Grids & Templates | Anti-Grid & Depth | 3D / Spatial |

---

### 2. Scrollytelling Mechanics — Specific Award-Winning Techniques

**Core Principle — Scroll as Playback Head:** Scroll input is decoupled from vertical translation. Instead of moving *down* a page, the user moves *through* a narrative space. GSAP ScrollTrigger or Lenis "hijack" scroll to scrub animation timelines — compressing vast narrative information into a single viewport, revealed sequentially in time rather than spatially in height.

Three named techniques winning awards:

**A. Scrolly-Driving (Cartier model)**
- Scroll controls a WebGL scene directly
- Inertia smoothing via Lenis interpolates raw scroll input into weighted, buttery camera pans
- Creates "physical mass" — a jerky trackpad swipe becomes a luxury camera movement
- The object feels heavy and substantial because interaction physics *resist* rapid erratic movement

**B. Liquid Transitions (Ponpon Mania model)**
- HTML images mapped to WebGL planes
- Fragment shaders manipulate texture coordinates based on scroll *velocity*
- Fast scroll = more distortion (communicates narrative energy); stopped scroll = image settles
- Achieved via gpu-curtains or curtains.js
- "Scroll triggers" lock story advancement — user cannot skip ahead, forcing engagement

**C. Gamified Narrative (Ponpon Mania)**
- Traditional comic panel structure broken down — characters physically move across the screen as user scrolls
- Speech bubbles pop with elastic physics
- Backgrounds shift in parallax depth
- Passive reading becomes active "playing" of the comic
- GSAP choreographs DOM elements; custom WebGL handles liquid distortions between scenes

**Cognitive psychology basis:** Scrollytelling serializes information — one concept at a time. "Pause points" where scroll locks and an animation plays act as cognitive punctuation, improving retention and reducing cognitive load vs. dense grid layouts.

---

### 3. Case Studies — What Specifically Makes Them Work

**Cartier Watches & Wonders (SOTM August 2025, dev: Immersive Garden)**

Three-layer scroll interaction controlling a full WebGL scene:
1. *3D Model Rotation* — scroll spins watch models for 360-degree inspection that feels manual, not automated
2. *Cinematic Focus Racking* — virtual camera depth-of-field shifts as user scrolls deeper, blurring background to isolate specific complications of the movement
3. *Macro-to-Micro Transitions* — seamless scroll transition between broad lifestyle imagery and microscopic product details, maintaining continuous visual lineage

The secret weapon is inertia smoothing (Lenis). A jerky trackpad input becomes a weighted camera pan. The digital object feels *heavy* — luxury is communicated through physics resistance, not just aesthetics. The brand enforces a pause on specific details (glint of a bezel, texture of leather strap) because scroll controls their pacing. The user is not looking at a watch; they are virtually handling it.

**Ponpon Mania (SOTM November 2025, by Patrick Heng)**

The question it answers: "What if a comic could breathe through web interaction?" Technical stack: GSAP for DOM choreography + custom WebGL (gpu-curtains/curtains.js) for liquid distortions. Key innovations:
- Liquid transition: images melt and reform into new scenes — fragment shaders manipulate texture coordinates based on scroll velocity
- Scroll triggers advance the plot (cannot skip) — creates lean-in experience
- Characters move across screen with elastic physics on speech bubbles
- Direct correlation between user input energy and visual output energy is the defining hallmark

**Lando Norris site (by OFF+BRAND)**

Typography "sandwiching" technique: text layers sit *behind and in front* of the 3D helmet model simultaneously. Text and helmet move at different parallax speeds, reinforcing the 3D environment. Cursor interaction explores "blobs" and interactive 3D assets — cursor becomes the user's avatar. Magnetic button physics throughout.

**Terminal Industries (SOTM September 2025, by REJOUICE)**

Spatial UI model: 3D renderings of trucks, containers, logistical yards are interactive assets responding to scroll and mouse. Camera flies through the scene (macro global supply chain to micro specific container). UI elements (text, buttons, data points) are *pinned to specific coordinates in 3D space* — they move with the camera, maintaining perspective and occlusion. Creates Mixed Reality feel in browser. Built on Three.js + GSAP.

**Navigate (by Resn, SOTD April 2025)**

Web3 data platform visualized as 3D objects in a "surrealist, programmable playground." Navigation is spatial exploration (flying between nodes) rather than hierarchical link-clicking. Three.js + mobile gyroscope/accelerometer inputs — tilting the phone lets users "look around" the 3D scene.

**Paper Tiger (2025 SOTY Nominee)**

Variable font "breathing" layout: headers expand in width to fill the screen or compress to make room for imagery as user scrolls. The type doesn't just wrap; it morphs. Solves responsive design challenges by turning the constraint into a feature.

**Midlife Engineering (SOTY Nominee, by Lee Black)**

Spatial Audio as design element — built in Framer + React. Visual element placement corresponds to sound placement in the stereo field. Moving an element left pans audio left; moving it back adds reverb (distance simulation). "Synesthetic Design" — appeals to multiple senses simultaneously. Demonstrates high-end spatial experience accessible via no-code/low-code platforms when React component logic is robust.

---

### 4. Tech Stack Co-occurrence Matrix

From the matrix chart (GSAP at highest co-occurrence across all pairings):

**Dominant stack: GSAP + Three.js + WebGL + React**
- GSAP-Three.js: highest co-occurrence (darkest blue) — the canonical pairing for full 3D sites
- GSAP-WebGL: very high — GSAP hooks into Three.js object properties (camera.position.z, mesh.rotation.y) to animate them based on scroll position
- GSAP-React: high — SOTM winners use React/Next.js as the framework shell
- Three.js-WebGL: inherent pairing (Three.js abstracts WebGL)
- Barba.js: strong co-occurrence with GSAP, Three.js, WebGL, React — used for page transition orchestration
- OGL: lower co-occurrence, used as a lighter-weight alternative when full Three.js overhead is not needed (2D-heavy immersive sites)
- Lenis: rising prominence specifically for scroll smoothing, pairs with GSAP

**The bifurcation:**
- Full 3D worlds (Terminal Industries, Navigate, Cartier): GSAP + Three.js (or R3F) + WebGL + Barba.js
- Enhanced 2D / liquid distortion sites (Ponpon Mania): GSAP + OGL/curtains.js + WebGL (no Three.js overhead)

**React Three Fiber (R3F)** is the key democratizing layer: `<mesh />`, `<material />`, `<light />` as standard JSX tags — lowered barrier from "Creative Coder" specialist to broader frontend workforce.

**GSAP's specific dominance:** ScrollTrigger plugin handles pinning (element fixed while scrolling), scrubbing (linking animation progress to scroll bar), and complex timeline sequencing native CSS cannot match. Present in nearly every SOTM winner. Ecosystem: Flip plugin (seamless layout transitions) + Observer (normalizing scroll events).

**No-Code ceiling:** Webflow and Framer generate GSAP code under the hood — raise the floor. But top-tier SOTY still requires custom GLSL shaders. "No-code raises the floor; code raises the ceiling."

---

### 5. Kinetic Typography — Text as Protagonist

Four taxonomy categories (from the report's visual):

1. **Variable Width** — font weight/width axes animate in real-time. Headers breathe, expand, compress with scroll or viewport. Paper Tiger exemplifies this — responsive design solved by morphing type, not wrapping it.

2. **Liquid Shader** — text rendered via WebGL (MSDF: Multi-Channel Signed Distance Fields) becomes a texture mesh. Fragment shaders applied directly to the letterforms. Studios Resn and Locomotive: text skews/blurs with fast scroll (motion blur), ripples like water, or disintegrates into particles ("Liquid Typography").

3. **3D Depth** — text sandwiched *within* the 3D scene (not overlaid on top). Lando Norris site: text layers behind and in front of the 3D helmet at different parallax speeds. Differentiator between a "flat" website and a "spatial" website.

4. **Particle Disintegration / Staggered Reveal** — character-by-character animation (staggered reveal), or text dissolves into particles on trigger.

**Maximalist Typography shift:** Massive screen-filling grotesque sans-serifs or sharp editorial serifs (Canela, Editorial New, Ogg). Set uppercase, tightly kerned — a graphic block that anchors the page. Motion design then softens the brutalist aesthetic.

**Serif Comeback ("Expressive Editorial"):** Canela, Ogg, and bespoke serifs inject warmth and editorial authority. The contrast between the rigid classical serif structure and fluid kinetic motion creates "heritage + future tech" tension. Dominant in Luxury/Fashion category (Watches & Wonders).

---

### 6. Physics-Based Micro-Interactions

**The Magnetic Button Phenomenon** — evolved from niche experiment to industry standard for high-end navigation. Found in Dolsten & Co, Lando Norris site, and essentially every SOTM winner.

**Mathematics of Magnetism (three core variables):**
- *Trigger Radius:* Distance from button center at which magnetic effect engages
- *Attraction Strength / Force Factor:* Coefficient of 0.3–0.7 — how closely button follows cursor
- *Spring Tension:* Force pulling button back to origin when cursor leaves radius

Translation formula: `Translation_x = (x_mouse - x_button) × ForceFactor`

The return to origin uses an **Elastic Ease function** (not linear) — simulates a physical spring oscillating and settling. This oscillation is what registers as "playful" and "responsive" to the human brain. Objects in the natural world do not stop instantly; they settle into equilibrium.

Dual purpose:
- *Functional:* Increases effective click area (Fitts's Law)
- *Emotional:* Creates a "handshake" — site acknowledges user's presence *before* they click

**Liquid Hover / Distortion Effects (WebGL-exclusive):**
- Mouse coordinates passed to shader as a uniform
- Shader calculates a "trail" or velocity map based on mouse speed/direction
- Trail displaces texture coordinates of the underlying image
- Distortion is viscous — ripples linger and slowly settle (implies digital surface has "thickness" like water, oil, or glass)
- Prime exemplar: Vadim Tyurin's liquid image effect hover

**Stateful Custom Cursors:**
- Default system cursor hidden; replaced by custom "follower" cursors that are contextual
- Watches & Wonders: cursor changes from small dot → "Drag" indicator over rotatable 3D watch → "Play" button over video areas
- Lando Norris: cursor is the user's avatar for exploring blobs and 3D assets
- Future trajectory: "predictive cursors" that subtly highlight where user *might* go next based on trajectory analysis

**Accessibility:** Best sites (Ponpon Mania cited specifically) implement `prefers-reduced-motion` checks. When enabled, magnetic effects, liquid distortions, and parallax scrolling all disable — reverting to accessible static layout. "Graceful Degradation" is cited as a hallmark of true digital craftsmanship.

---

**Source:** Awwwards 2026 Digital Craftsmanship Report — "The Era of Expressive Utility." Data sourced from Awwwards SOTM Oct 24–Dec 25, SOTY 2025 Nominees, and Willy Brauner Journal. All 17 pages read in full.

## Agent 102: React Server Components: Latest Patterns 2026

*Source: aec5b8b6982b43b00.output*

I'll search for the latest React Server Components patterns and related developments in 2026.
## React Server Components: Latest Patterns 2026

**Key Findings:**

### RSC Core Patterns
- **Default-to-Server**: Use Server Components by default; add 'use client' only at leaf nodes (buttons, form inputs)
- **Data Sharing**: Use React's cache() for memoization across components in a single request, avoiding prop drilling
- **Context Pattern**: Client Component providers nested in Server Components, receiving initial data via props
- **Streaming Architecture**: Static shell + dynamic Suspense islands; parallel data fetching prevents waterfalls

### Partial Prerendering (Stable)
Next.js 16 has graduated PPR from experimental to stable via **Cache Components**. PPR is now the recommended default rendering strategy, combining static and dynamic content for optimal performance. The experimental.ppr flag has been removed.

### Architecture Best Practices
- Colocate data fetching with consuming components
- Avoid placing too much logic in Client Components (bloats bundles)
- Pass Server Components as children to Client Components (not imports)
- Target 60-70% bundle size reduction vs traditional SPAs
- Minimum: React 18.2+, Next.js 14+

### Key Pattern for 2026 Agents
Encode **"push 'use client' to leaf level"** + **PPR for dynamic routes** + **parallel data fetching** as default architectural recommendations.

**Sources:**
- [Next.js Partial Prerendering Docs](https://nextjs.org/docs/15/app/getting-started/partial-prerendering)
- [Next.js 16 Release](https://nextjs.org/blog/next-16)
- [Mastering RSC 2026](https://www.kunal-chowdhury.com/2026/03/react-server-components.html)
- [RSC Best Practices](https://www.sachith.co.uk/react-server-components-what-to-adopt-now-best-practices-in-2025-practical-guide-mar-14-2026/)

## Mission 33 — Emerging Interaction Paradigms

**Findings:**

The web in 2026 is mid-transition from scroll/click toward four converging paradigms:

1. **Spatial / WebXR hand tracking**: WebXR has become production-grade on Apple Vision Pro and Meta Quest Browser. No controllers — pure hand gesture navigation. The API exposes 25 skeleton joints per hand. Combined with WebGPU, XR experiences are closing the gap with native apps rapidly.

2. **Haptic feedback (multisensory web)**: The `navigator.vibrate()` API is now widely used via npm packages like `web-haptics`. The Web Haptics API (Microsoft Edge Explainer) proposes declarative haptics for scroll-snap carousels, form validation, panel transitions — still no W3C standard but rapidly gaining traction. Mobile sites use subtle vibrations for form success/error states.

3. **Zero-UI / gesture navigation**: Sites are moving beyond menus and buttons toward natural hand gestures, voice commands, and environmental cues. "Post-click" era is the framing being used — swipe/pinch replacing tap, voice replacing search bars.

4. **Scroll as explicit narrative tool**: 2026 scrollytelling wraps scroll back as intentional UX, not gimmick — the scroll is positioned as the user's pacing mechanism for journeys, not just vertical movement.

**Sources:**
- [Web Haptics NPM Package — Medium, Mar 2026](https://medium.com/@springmusk/web-haptics-the-npm-package-everyones-adding-for-haptic-feedback-4c774f10caaa)
- [Innovative Website Design Trends for 2026](https://web999.in/innovative-website-design-trends-for-2026/)
- [Web Haptics API — Microsoft Edge Explainers](https://microsoftedge.github.io/MSEdgeExplainers/Haptics/explainer.html)
- [Beyond Visual: Haptic Feedback on the Web — DEV Community](https://dev.to/luxonauta/beyond-visual-why-we-should-be-using-more-haptic-feedback-on-the-web-1adg)
- [WebXR Browser Immersive Experiences 2026 — MadXR](https://www.madxr.io/webxr-browser-immersive-experiences-2026.html)
- [Spatial Computing & Real-World Testing: 2026 Developer's Playbook — Medium](https://medium.com/@instatunnel/spatial-computing-real-world-testing-the-2026-developers-playbook-57b883edff75)
- [Spatial Interact: The Next Frontier — INAIRSPACE](https://inairspace.com/blogs/learn-with-inair/spatial-interact-the-next-frontier-in-human-computer-connection)
- [UX Trends 2026 — Medium](https://medium.com/@mohitphogat/the-ux-trends-2026-designers-need-to-know-not-just-guess-3269d023b0b7)

**Relevance to web-god:** The scrollytelling skill is web-god's core domain, but it is now one paradigm among several. Haptic punctuation of scroll milestones (vibrate on chapter transitions) is achievable today on mobile with zero dependencies. WebXR hand-gesture navigation is a future expansion surface for the choreographer agent.

**Action:** Add `navigator.vibrate()` haptic milestone pulses to the scrollytelling skill's mobile output (e.g., chapter transitions, progress gates). Document WebXR as a future domain. Priority: **P2**, Effort: **S**

## Mission 34 — AI-Generated Animation

Supports React, Next.js, WordPress. Free tier with per-generation metering; premium effects waitlist open.
